It instructs us that every Christian ought to submit himself and be obedient to the Church.

This Christ himself teaches, If he neglects to hear the Church, let him be to you as a heathen and a tax collector (Matthew 18:17).

And the Church is so fully empowered, that she has authority, in her Ecumenical Councils, to examine (δοκιμάζῃ; Latin: examinare) and confirm (approbare) the Scriptures; to inquire into the behaviour and so judge of Patriarchs, Popes, Bishops, and all others; and to inflict severe canonical punishment upon them according to their offences. For it is the pillar and the ground of truth, as the Apostle says:

That you may know how you ought to behave yourself in the House of God, which is the Church of the Living God, the Pillar and Ground of Truth. (1 Timothy 3:15)

The Precepts of the Church.

87. What are the precepts of the Church?

The chief and principal commands or precepts of the Church are nine in number:

Reverent worship and regular attendance at Church. 

Seasonal fasts of the Church Year. 

Honor to clergy. 

Regular confession. 

Avoidance of corrupt doctrines and heretics. 

Intercessory prayer for all peoples. 

Occasional prayers and fasts. 

Tithes and offerings. 

Consistent Christian worldview and lifestyle. 

Of the first precept, That every one worship God with an humble and contrite heart, and carefully observe all the Sundays and Holy Days, and all other solemn times as the Church shall appoint. That is, by diligently attending the services, of Matins, the Liturgy, Vespers, and instruction (διδαχή; Latin: concionem), of which the Scripture thus teaches us, Men ought always to pray, and not to faint (Luke 18:1). And again:

Praying always with all prayer and supplication in the Spirit, and watching thereunto with all perseverance and supplication for all saints. (Ephesians 6:18)

And in another place St. Paul goes on, Pray without ceasing (1 Thessalonians 5:17).

88. What is the second precept of the Church?

The second precept of the Church is that every Christian yearly keep and observe four stated or set fasts, i.e. periods of fasting.

The first is the time leading up to the Nativity of Christ, which is to say the Nativity Fast, beginning the fifteenth day of November.

The second, which is called Great Lent, prior to Pascha, is established by the fasting of Christ himself, And when he had fasted forty days and forty nights, at length he was hungry (Matthew 4:2).

The third is of the holy Apostles, which the Church appoints to begin immediately after the week of Pentecost (on the second Monday after the Sunday of Pentecost); this is called the Apostles’s Fast, because at this time the holy Apostles, prior to sending forth Paul and Barnabas to preach the Gospel, solemnised a fast, as appears from Acts, When they had fasted and prayed, they laid their hands on them and sent them away (Acts 13:3).

The fourth is the Dormition Fast, the fast leading up to the falling asleep of the most holy Theotokos and Ever-Virgin Mary, beginning the first day of August, and ending on the Feast of the Dormition, which is the fifteenth day of the same month.

Moreover, Orthodox Christians must fast on all Wednesdays and Fridays, but not on Saturdays and Sundays (this being forbidden by the 66th Canon of the holy Apostles),[124] except the great Sabbath, or holy Saturday, which is the Eve of Pascha.

The Church also appoints the fourteenth of September, being the Exaltation of the Holy Cross, to be observed as a fast, because on that day we solemnise the memory of our Lord’s suffering, by reciting the Gospel wherein the Passion of Jesus Christ is related.

Also, the twenty-ninth of August is to be held a fast, it being the beheading of St. John the Baptist, the Forerunner of Christ.

Furthermore, on some certain days of exception the Church forbids us to fast: First, from the day of our Lord's Nativity to holy Epiphany; and, secondly, all of Bright Week (Greek: διακαινήσμος ἑβδομάς, or, Renewal Week) following, and including, Pascha; and, thirdly, the week of Pentecost (or, Trinity Week); also, fourthly, the week beginning on the Sunday of the Publican and the Pharisee.

All which the orthodox Christian ought most faithfully to keep and observe.

89. What is the third precept of the Church?

The third precept is that we respect spiritual persons with all due reverence, as the ministers of God set apart, who pray to God for us: Especially those who, as our spiritual fathers, receive our confessions, and whom we are to consult about our salvation.

Of this command the Scripture thus declares, Let a man so account of us, as of the ministers of Christ, and stewards of the mysteries of God (1 Corinthians 4:1). And again:

We beseech you, brethren, to know them who labor among you, and are over you in the Lord, and admonish you that you esteem them very highly in love, for their work’s sake. (1 Thessalonians 5:12-13)

Also in another place:

Do you not know that those who minister the holy things live by the things of the Temple, and those who serve at the Altar are partakers with the Altar? Even so the Lord has ordained that those who preach the Gospel should live from the Gospel. (1 Corinthians 9:13-14)

Also in his Epistle to Timothy, Let the Elders that rule well be counted worthy of double honor, especially they who labor in the word and doctrine (1 Timothy 5:17).

It is, therefore, unlawful for laypersons to meddle with and thrust themselves into spiritual matters; as the Apostle says: Brethren, if a man be overtaken in a fault, you who are spiritual restore such a one in the spirit of gentleness (Galatians 6:1).

90. What is the fourth precept of the Church?

The fourth precept is that we make Confession of our sins to a Priest, rightly and lawfully ordained, four times every year.

However, those who are well advanced in religion and godliness may discharge this duty every month. The weaker brethren must at least once a year, namely, during Great Lent, not fail to unburden their conscience by Confession: And let the sick, before all things, purify their conscience by Confession, and partaking of holy Communion, having first received the holy oil, with prayer, with reverence and devotion.

91. What is the fifth precept of the Church?

The fifth precept is that plain and unlearned men do not read the books of heretics, nor listen to their doctrines, nor entertain any conversation with them, lest they be corrupted, by becoming familiar with them: As the Psalmist and Prophet warns us, Blessed is the man who has not walked in the counsel of the ungodly, nor stood in the way of sinners (Psalm 1:1). And elsewhere the Scripture enjoins us, A man that is a heretic, after the first and second admonition, reject (Titus 3:10).

92. What is the sixth precept of the Church?

The sixth precept is that we pray unto the all-good God for all conditions of the people.

First, For all spiritual persons; for the most holy Patriarch, for the Metropolitan, and for the Bishop of the province to which we belong.

Secondly, For the King (or, the highest civil authority), the Governor of our province (or, local territory), the Senate (or, civil authorities) and Commonwealth (or, country or nation), and for the military (or, armed forces); but chiefly for those who deserve well of the Church and endeavour to propagate and extend the orthodox and catholic religion. According to the Apostle:

I exhort, therefore, that first of all supplications, prayers, intercessions, and giving of thanks be made for all men, for kings, and for all that are in authority, that we may lead a quiet and peaceable life, in all godliness and honesty; for this is good and acceptable in the sight of God our Savior. (1 Timothy 2:1-3)

Thirdly, We are to pray for all those who are departed hence in the orthodox Faith.

Lastly, We are to make intercession for heretics and schismatics, that they may repent and return to the sanctuary of true religion before their last day.

93. What is the seventh precept of the Church?

The seventh precept is that all people observe to keep the fasts and supplications which are particularly enjoined and appointed by their Metropolitan or Bishops of their Diocese, when they think proper, by reason of any necessity, to appease the wrath of God, afflicting his people; and for deliverance from pestilence, famine, war, drought, excessive rain, and the like: Or for the sick, and those that are oppressed. As it is said, Peter therefore was kept in prison: but prayer was made without ceasing of the Church to God for him (Acts 12:5).

94. What is the eighth precept of the Church?

The eighth precept is that lay persons (κοσμικοὶ) do not presume to meddle with the goods or finances belonging to the Church, or take them away by fraud or force and apply them to their own needs; but that those cares be committed to the leaders of the Church, that with the holy treasure they may provide vestments and other necessities; so that those who minister unto the Church, being clothed and fed, they may have wherewith to relieve the poor and the stranger: According to the Scripture:

The disciples, everyone according to his ability, determined to send relief unto the brethren that dwelt in Judea; which also they did, and sent it to the Elders by the hands of Barnabas and Saul. (Acts 11:29-30)

Besides, it is unjust that either lay persons (κοσμικοὺς), or, indeed, that even the spiritual leaders who, in any Church, are entrusted with the care of the things appertaining to the Church, should withdraw any money, or other valuable movables, whether left by will to the Church or given to it otherwise, and pervert them to their own private use and profit: For thus the piety of well-disposed persons would be disappointed and rendered ineffectual.

95. What is the ninth precept of the Church?

That no marriage be solemnised on such days as are forbidden by the Church.

Also, that orthodox Christians abstain from forbidden games and diversions, and from theaters and unseemly shows; not following the morality (τὰ ἢθη) of the barbarians (τὰ βάρβαρα, i.e. those foreign to or ignorant of Christianity), but avoiding, as much as possible, all their bad customs.

96. Why do we, who ought only to believe in God, profess to believe in the Church, which is a creation?

Because, although the Church is a creation, as consisting of men (who are created beings), she has for her Head Christ himself, who is very God. She has the Holy Spirit, who perpetually teaches and guides her, and makes her, according to the Apostle, The spotless and unblemished Spouse of Christ (Ephesians 5:27); and, The pillar and ground of the truth (1 Timothy 3:15).

Now, her decrees (δόγματα) and doctrines (διδάγματά) are by no means human, but divine; not by man, but of God. When, therefore, we profess to believe in her, we profess to believe in the words of God (i.e. the Scriptures) traditioned to her (θεόπαράδοτά της λόγια), and in the decrees which are inspired by God (θεόπνευστα δόγματα). For thus says the Scripture, Holy men of God spoke as they were moved by the Holy Spirit (2 Peter 1:21). In like manner St. Paul, You received it not as the word of men, but as it is in truth, the word of God, which also effectually works in you who believe (1 Thessalonians 2:13).

Wherefore we are persuaded to have faith, both in the holy Gospel, which the Church has received, according to that command of Christ himself, Believe the Gospel (Mark 1:15), and also in all the other holy writings and decrees of Councils.

c3B8

The Tenth Article: Of the Sacraments.

97. What is the Tenth Article of the Faith?

I confess one Baptism for the remission of sins.

98. What does this Article of the Faith teach?

Whereas we here have Baptism, which is the first Sacrament (τὸ πρῶτον μυστήριον) of the Church made mention of: This seems a fitting place wherein to discourse concerning the Seven Sacraments of the Church, which are these:[125]

Baptism 

The Oil of Anointing (Chrismation) 

The Eucharist (Communion) 

Holy Orders (Ordination) 

Repentance (Confession, and Absolution) 

Honorable Marriage 

The Anointing of the Sick 

These Seven Sacraments correspond unto the Seven Gifts of the Spirit inasmuch as by them the Holy Spirit conveys his Gifts and grace unto the soul that rightly uses these Sacraments. Concerning which matter the Patriarch Jeremiah has more at large treated, in the books which he wrote for the conversion of the Lutherans.[126]

99. What is a Sacrament (μυστήριον)?

A Sacrament (μυστήριον) is a certain holy rite or ceremony, which, under a visible element, causes and conveys into the soul of the faithful the invisible grace of God; an institution of our Lord, whereby every one of the faithful receives the divine grace.

100. What things are requisite to a Sacrament?

Three things.

First, Fit and proper material (ὓλη), as water in Baptism, bread and wine in the Eucharist, oil and other things in their respective Sacraments.

Secondly, A Bishop, or a Priest, regularly ordained.

Thirdly, The invocation of the Holy Spirit, and a solemn form of Words: By which the Priest celebrates the Sacrament, through the power and efficacy of the Holy Spirit: Having a fixed and deliberate intention of celebrating it.

101. For what end were the Sacraments instituted?

First, That they might be the marks and signs of the true Children of God, and of the orthodox, catholic, and Apostolic Church. For whosoever rightly partakes of the Sacraments, the same is a real and true member of the Church of God; and by grace a Child of God.

Secondly, That we might have a sure pledge of our trust in God. To wit, that if we persevere with constancy in faith, and with good works following (καὶ μετὰ καλὰ ἒργα), then we shall become partakers of salvation and eternal life.

Thirdly, That we might have a present and certain remedy wherewith to heal the diseases caused by our sins.

102. What is the first Sacrament, or Baptism?

Baptism is a washing away and rooting out of the Ancestral Sin (τοῦ προπατορικοῦ ἁμαρτὴματος; Latin: peccati originalis),[127] by being thrice immersed in water, the Priest pronouncing these words: In the Name of the Father, Amen; and of the Son, Amen; and of the Holy Spirit, Amen.[128]

After which regeneration (ἀναγέννησιν; Latin: regenerationem) by water and the Spirit a man is restored to the grace of God, and the way opened him into the Kingdom of Heaven; as our Savior said, Except a man is born of water and of the Spirit, he cannot enter into the Kingdom of God (John 3:5).

But this Sacrament, being once received, is not to be again repeated; provided the person who administered the Baptism believed according to an orthodox manner in three Persons in One God, and accurately, and without any alteration, pronounced the aforementioned words; namely, In the Name of the Father, and of the Son, and of the Holy Spirit, Amen, as the holy, catholic, and orthodox Church directs.

103. What is requisite to be observed in this Sacrament?

First, It is necessary that the infant, by his Sponsor, who must be an Orthodox Christian, should renounce the Devil and all his works, and all respect and worship to him: But if the person to be baptized is of legal age (νομίμου ἡλικίας), then he shall, with his own mouth, make this renunciation, by answering the question put to him by the Priest, and by expressing his scorn of Satan and all his works.

Secondly, That he make open confession of the Faith, by repeating the Symbol of the Faith (τὸ σύμβολον τῆς πίστεως) i.e. the Creed; but if it be an infant, the Sponsor shall profess the Symbol of the Faith (τὸ σύμβολον τοοῦτο τῆς πίστεως) for it, and bind (ὑποσχεθῇ) it unto Christ.

And, thirdly, That care be taken to use none other than pure water, not mixed with any other thing; and such as is natural, and not made by art (τεχνητὸν).

Furthermore, lawful Baptism must necessarily be administered by a minister of the Word only, unless in case of urgent necessity, when any other person, whether man or woman, may administer this Sacrament; being observant to use the proper requisite, namely, unmixed and natural and pure water, and dipping the person to be baptized thrice therein, repeating the solemn form of In the Name of the Father, and of the Son, and of the Holy Spirit, Amen. And this Baptism, which is not to be repeated again, is of such power and efficacy, that it is an undoubted seal and pledge of eternal salvation.

Now, what the fruits and benefits are of this Sacrament may be easily apprehended; for, first, it takes away Ancestral (προπατορικό) Sin in infants, and in those of older age it takes away both original (προπατορικό) and voluntary (προαιρετικός) sin.

Secondly, It renews (ἀνακαινίζεται) the man, and restores (αποκαθίσταται) him to that state of justice and righteousness wherein he stood before, innocent and sinless; as the Apostle testifies, But you are washed, but you are sanctified, but you are justified, in the Name of the Lord Jesus, and by the Spirit of our God (1 Corinthians 6:11).

Moreover, by Baptism we become members of the Body of Christ, and put on the Lord, as the Apostle bears witness, As many of you as have been baptized with Christ have put on Christ (Galatians 3:27).

104. What is the second Sacrament in the Church of Christ?

The Oil of Anointing (τὸ μύρον τοῦ χρίσματος; i.e. Chrismation) is the second Sacrament; and this had its beginning at the time when the Holy Spirit came down from Heaven and rested upon the Apostles (Acts 2:1-4), and sealed them with his divine grace, that they might preach the Faith of Christ steadfastly and without ceasing.

Of this blessing and divine assistance everyone has need who becomes a Christian; and as then the Holy Spirit came down in the visible form of fire and bestowed his grace, or Gifts, upon the Apostles, so now, when the Priest anoints (i.e. Chrismates) the newly baptized person with the holy Oil, he becomes endued from above with the Gifts of the Holy Spirit: As appears from the words which the Priest, as appointed, uses in the celebration of this Sacrament; namely, The Seal of the Gift of the Holy Spirit, Amen. As if he should say, “By the anointing of this holy Oil you are sealed and confirmed into the Gifts of the Holy Spirit, which you receive for a confirmation of your Christian faith.” Agreeable hereto are the words of the Apostle:

He who establishes us with you in Christ, and has anointed us, is God: Who has also sealed us, and given the pledge of the Spirit in our hearts. (2 Corinthians 1:21-22)

This Anointing (i.e. Chrismation), or rather the bestowing the efficacy of this Anointing, was done in the times of the Apostles by laying on of hands; according to the Scripture, Then they laid their hands on them, and they received the Holy Spirit (Acts 8:17). This was afterwards performed by Anointing with Oil, as we learn from St. Dionysius the Areopagite, who was the disciple of St. Paul (The Ecclesiastical Hierarchy, chapters 2 and 4).

105. How many things are necessary for the Sacrament?

First, It is necessary that this Oil (μύρον) be consecrated by the highest Bishop. Secondly, That it be composed of apt and fit material, such as oils, balms, and other scents. Thirdly, That immediately after Baptism the Priest do anoint the members of the baptized person which are directed to be anointed, using this form of words, The Seal of the Gift of the Holy Spirit, Amen.

Now, from this Sacrament do arise the following Fruits: First, That as by Baptism we are born again (ἀναἑννώμεθα), so by this holy Oil we are made partakers (μέτοχοι) of the Holy Spirit, we are confirmed or established in the Faith of the Lord, and immediately become ripe in the divine favor; As the Apostle teaches us:

He saved us, not by works of righteousness which we have accomplished, but according to his mercy, by the washing of regeneration, and renewing of the Holy Spirit, which he shed on us abundantly, through Jesus Christ our Savior. (Titus 3:5-6)

Secondly, That by the power (δύναμις) of the Holy Spirit we are so strengthened and established, that our spiritual Enemy cannot, by any means, hurt our souls.

Lastly, This Sacrament may not again be repeated unless it be unto those who, having renounced the Name of Christ, return again unto the profession of his true Faith.

106. What is the third Sacrament?

The holy Eucharist, or the Body and Blood of our Lord Jesus Christ, under the beholding (ὑποκάτο εἰς τὴν θεωρίαν; Latin: sub visibili specie) of bread and wine: Wherein, according to the thing itself (ἤγουν κατὰ τὸ πρᾶγμα), Jesus Christ is truly and principally present (ἀληθῶς καὶ κυρίως παρών).

This Sacrament is in an eminent degree above all the other Sacraments, and conduces unto our salvation in a more, especial manner than they all do; for in this Sacrament the whole grace and benefits of our Lord Jesus are manifested and imparted to all the faithful; as will more fully appear hereafter.

107. What is to be observed in this Mystery?

First, That no one presume to administer this Sacrament, on any occasion whatsoever, although ever so urgent; but only a Priest lawfully ordained.

Secondly, It must be provided, that where the Priest is to celebrate this Sacrament, there be a fit and proper Altar, or at least a consecrated antimension (ἀντιμίσιον), without one of which he may not, by any means, offer the bloodless Sacrifice (τὴν ἀναίμακτον θυσίαν).

Thirdly, Let him take heed that he be provided with the proper necessaries, namely, the purest leavened bread, made of wheat, and the purest wine, unmixed with any other liquor: whereto, in the time of celebration, let water be added; that the Scripture may be fulfilled, which says, One of the soldiers pierced his side, and forthwith came there out blood and water (John 19:34).

Fourthly, In the moments of consecration of the holy Gifts, the Priest must firmly and undoubtingly resolve within himself that the substance or essence (οὐσία) of the bread and the substance or essence (οὐσία) of the wine are changed (μεταβάλλεται) into the substance or essence of the true Body and Blood of Christ (εἰς τὴν οὐσίαν τοῦ ἀληθινοῦ σώματος και αἵματος τοῦ Χριστοῦ), by the operation or working of the Holy Spirit (διὰ τῆς ἐνεργείς τοῦ ἁγίου Πνεύματος), whose power and influence let the Priest invoke (ἐπίκλησιν) in these words, in order to the due performance of this Sacrament:

O Lord, send down from Heaven your Holy Spirit upon us, and upon these Gifts here offered; and make this Bread the precious Body of your Christ, and that which is in this Cup the precious Blood of your Christ, changing (μεταβαλών) them by your Holy Spirit.

At these words there is wrought a change in the substance or essence of the elements (μετουσίωσις; Latin: Transsubstantiatio), and the bread becomes truly the Body of Christ, and the wine truly his Blood; the species (τὰ εἴδη) only remaining, which are perceived by the sight.

This is so, in the first place, that we might not behold the Body of Christ with our Eyes, but by faith, only, believe it to be so; because Christ said, This is my Body and This is my Blood, depending and trusting rather in the words and power of our Lord than on our own senses; and so becoming partakers of the blessing which is the reward of faith, Blessed are they that have not seen, and yet have believed (John 20:29).

And furthermore, secondly, because that human nature would abhor and be shocked at the eating of raw flesh; and yet, nevertheless, by the partaking of the Body and Blood of Christ in this Mystery, a Christian is most closely united with Christ. Therefore, to the end that our weak nature might not abominate and reject this necessary partaking, the divine goodness has taken this method to familiarise and conciliate it unto us, and imparts unto the faithful the very Body and the very Blood of Christ for food and drink, under the clothing of bread and wine (ὑποκάτω εἰς τὸ ἒνδυμα τοῦ ἂρτου καὶ τοῦ οἲνου). (Hereof St. Gregory of Nyssa and St. John of Damascus fully treat.)[129]

Moreover, the Communion of this Sacrament ought to be alike received and partaken by the clergy and the laity, by the Priest and the People, in both kinds; namely, of both the bread and the wine. For so Christ commanded, without making any distinction:

Except you eat the flesh of the Son of Man, and drink his blood, you have no life in you. Whoever eats my flesh and drinks my blood has eternal life, and I will raise him up on the last day. ... Whoso eats my flesh, and drinks my blood, abides in me, and I in him. (John 6:53-54, 56)

Wherefore the holy Apostles have delivered this Mystery unto others, as they themselves received it of Christ; namely, to the joint and equal use of both lay persons and persons in the Priesthood; and in both kinds, both of the bread and of the wine: As St. Paul writes to the Corinthians:

For I received of the Lord that which I also delivered unto you; that the Lord Jesus, in the same night in which he was betrayed, took bread; and when he had given thanks, he broke it, and said, “Take, eat; this is my body, which is broken for you; do this in remembrance of me.” After the same manner, also, he took the cup, after supper, saying, “This cup is the New Covenant in my blood; do this, as often as you drink of it, in remembrance of me.” (1 Corinthians 11:23)

Furthermore, the honor which is due to these awful Mysteries ought to be the same and equal to that which is rendered to Christ himself, and as St. Peter, speaking in the name of all the Apostles, and, as it were, with their mouth, said, You are the Christ, the Son of the living God (Matthew 16:16); so we, in like manner worshipping him, do profess, saying:

I believe, O Lord, and I confess, that you are truly the Christ, the Son of the living God, who came into the world to save sinners, of whom I am first.

This holy Sacrament is also offered as a sacrifice (θυσία) for all Orthodox Christians, both among the living and those who have fallen asleep in hope of a joyful resurrection; and this sacrifice (θυσία) shall never fail, nor be discontinued, even unto the end of the world.

The Chief Fruits of the Eucharist.

First, A commemoration (ἀνάμνησις) of the sinless suffering (τοῦ ἀναμαρτήτου πάθους) and death (καὶ τοῦ θανάτου) of Christ;[130] As says the Scripture, As often as you eat this bread and drink this cup, you proclaim the Lord’s death till he comes (1 Corinthians 11:26).

Secondly, This Sacrament is a propitiation (ἱλασμός) and reconciliation (καλοσύνημα; Latin: propitatio reconciliatioque) with God (πρὸς τὸν Θὲον), for our sins, both of the living and also of the dead: Wherefore the holy Liturgy is never without prayers and supplications made unto God for our sins.

Thirdly, The devout Christian, who is frequently present at this Sacrifice (θυσίαν) and Sacrament of Communion (κοινωνᾷ τοῦ μυστηρίου), is hereby delivered from the temptations and snares of the Devil; for that Enemy of the soul cannot prevail against him whom he finds to have Christ abiding in him.

Lastly, A man should rightly prepare himself for the receiving of this dread Mystery, according to the precepts of our Orthodox Church; namely, by a sincere confession of his sins, by fasting and mortification, by a perfect reconciliation with all persons, and the like.

108. What is the fourth Mystery?

Holy Orders, or the Priesthood; and this is twofold — the one spiritual, the other sacramental.

Of the former, namely, the spiritual Priesthood, all Christians in general are equally endowed, and do exercise it in common, according to that saying of St. Peter the Apostle, But you are a chosen race, a royal priesthood, a holy nation, a people for God’s own possession (1 Peter 2:9). And St. John, in Revelation:

You were slain, and have redeemed us to God by your blood, out of every tribe and tongue, and people and nation; and have made us unto our God kings and priests. (Revelation 5:9)

And accordingly as this priesthood is, so are its offerings; namely, prayers, thanksgiving, mortification of the flesh, voluntary sufferings of martyrdom for Christ, and such like; to which the Apostle St. Peter exhorts us:

You also, as living stones, are built up a spiritual house, a holy priesthood to offer up spiritual sacrifices, acceptable to God, by Jesus Christ. (1 Peter 2:5)

Also St. Paul:

I exhort you, therefore, brethren, by the mercies of God, that you present your bodies a living sacrifice, holy, acceptable unto God, which is your rational worship. (Romans 12:1)

109. What, then, is the sacramental priesthood?

The Priesthood, which is a Sacrament (μυστήριον), is that which Christ committed to his Apostles; which is continued down unto this day, by their laying on of hands, and by the laying on of hands of the Bishops, who are the successors of the Apostles, to dispense the divine Sacraments (θείων μυστηρίων), and to perform the ministry of the salvation of mankind. Thus says the Apostle, Let a man so account of us as of the ministers of Christ, and stewards of the mysteries of God (1 Corinthians 4:1).

Now, this Stewardship is twofold. First, it contains the power of absolving men from their sins, as we read in St. Matthew, Whatsoever you shall loose on earth, shall be loosed in heaven (Matthew 18:18). Secondly, it contains the authority and power to teach (ἡ ἐξουσία καὶ ἡ δύναμις τοῦ διδάσκειν); as it is delivered unto us in these Words:

Go therefore, and teach all nations, baptizing them in the Name of the Father, and of the Son, and of the Holy Spirit: Teaching them to observe all things whatsoever I have commanded you. (Matthew 28:19-20)

From hence it is manifest that Christ sent forth the Apostles to preach the Gospel; and again, the Apostles afterwards sent forth others, whom they ordained to this work by the laying on of their hands; according to St. Luke, Then they laid their hands on them, and they received the Holy Spirit (Acts 8:17). And again:

As they ministered (λειτουργέω) unto the Lord (that is, while they offered the unbloody Sacrifice to God), and fasted, the Holy Spirit said, “Separate to me Barnabas and Saul for the work whereunto I have called them”; and when they had fasted and prayed, and laid their hands on them, they sent them away. (Acts 13:2-3)

Also St. Paul, Lay hands suddenly on no man (1 Timothy 5:22). By this kind of ordination (τὴν χειροτονίαν) and uninterrupted succession (τὴν διαδοχὴιν τὴν μηδέποτε διακοπεῖσαν), those who are sent forth unto this work have the power to teach the saving doctrines (τὰ σωτηριώδη δόγματα); as says St. Paul, How shall they preach unless they are sent? (Romans 10:15)

110. What is to be regarded in this Sacrament?

Those who are admitted to this Sacrament must be first thoroughly examined and tried, that they be qualified in three particulars.

First, That they have a good and clear conscience, so that they are free from all those sins which render them incapable of receiving ordination.

Secondly, That they are adorned with knowledge and wisdom, both in dispensing the holy Mysteries, as also in edifying the common people by their teaching.

Thirdly, That they be sound and perfect in all their limbs and members; which are necessary toward a due discharge of their function.

111. Are there any degrees prior to the rank of the priesthood?

The priesthood includes all prior ranks in itself; nevertheless, they ought all to be given in their due order; namely, the Lector, or Reader; the Chanter, or Singer; the Lampadary, or he who lights the lamps; the Sub-Deacon; the Deacon; as more at large is declared in the Euchologion, where it directs concerning these things.

However, it is enough at present, with relation to teaching this orthodox confession, to note that it is the duty of the Bishop, that unto whatsoever degree of holy Orders he does admit any person, he do clearly and plainly instruct such person in the duties of that office which is committed to him, whether it be presiding at the the divine Liturgy, or reading the Gospel, or the Epistle, or carrying the sacred utensils, or cleaning the Church building. For each degree is distinguished from another by its own peculiar signs; and this is to be explained and taught them by the Bishop.

112. What is the fifth Sacrament?

The fifth Sacrament is Repentance (μετάνοια), which is a true and thorough sorrow in the heart for the sin that a person has been guilty of, and which he confessed unto the Priest, his spiritual father, with a full purpose of performing the penance (ἐπιτιμήσει) given by him, and a steadfast resolution to amend his life for the future.

This Mystery is most complete, and does then most fully benefit us, when, according to the constitution and customs (τὴν τάξιν καὶ ζυνήθειαν) of the Church, absolution or forgiveness (συγχώρησίν) of sins is granted by the Priest. For so soon as any person receives the absolution of his sins, they are all forthwith pardoned by God, through the ministration of the Priest: According to the Words of Christ, Receive the Holy Spirit. If you forgive the sins of any, they are forgiven; and if you retain the sins of any, they are retained (John 20:22-23).

113. What is to be observed in this Mystery?

First, It is requisite that the repentant (μετανοῶν) be a Christian of the Orthodox and catholic faith; for that repentance which is destitute of a right faith is no repentance, nor is it acceptable with God.

Secondly, That the Confessor who hears and receives the repentance of Christians be also, himself, Orthodox; for an heretic and an apostate has no power of absolution.

Thirdly, It is necessary that the repentant have a contrite heart and a serious sorrow for his sins, whereby he has provoked God to anger or injured his neighbor. Concerning which contrition David said, A broken and contrite heart, O God, you will not despise (Psalm 51:17).

This contrition of heart ought to be followed by an exact and particular confession, in words, of all sins and misdeeds; for the spiritual father cannot absolve where he knows of nothing to absolve; neither can he lay on or impose due penance (ἐπιτίμιον).

The holy Scripture manifestly speaks of this confession, And many that believed came, and confessed, and showed their deeds (Acts 19:18). And elsewhere, Confess your faults one to another, and pray one for another, that you may be healed (James 5:16). And:

And there went out unto him all the land of Judea, and they 0f Jerusalem, and were all baptized of him in the River Jordan, confessing their sins. (Mark 1:5)

Now, this confession ought to have these properties, namely, that whenever it is exercised it be humble, devout, truthful, sincere, accusing itself with grief and mourning.

The fourth part of repentance is the penitential rule (κανόνας) and the restorative penalty (ἐπιτίμιον), which the Confessor defines and appoints (δίδῃ καὶ διορίζῃ; Latin: definit et imponit); of which kind are prayer, charitable giving, fastings, pilgrimages to holy places, prostrations, and the like; as the spiritual father judges to be proper.

And let him who departs from Confession, having obtained Absolution, apply to himself what the author of the Psalms says, Eschew evil, and do good (Psalm 34:14). And also that which our Savior says, Behold you are made whole: sin no more, lest a worse thing come upon you (John 5:14). And again, Go, and sin no more (John 8:11).

But, forasmuch as it is impossible that a man should altogether and entirely avoid sin, the pious Christian must, according to the best of his understanding and ability, endeavour, from confession to confession, to amend his life to the utmost of his power.

114. What are the fruits of this Mystery?

The first benefit which we receive is that, whereas by sin we are deprived of that innocence we obtained in Baptism, now, by Repentance, we approach nearer to that state.

And as, by sin, we had forfeited the divine grace, now, by Repentance, we regain it: And, having become the slaves of the Devil by sin, by Repentance we are made free.

Lastly, as fear and dread entered into our consciences through sin, so now, by Repentance, we are restored to peace and courage, such as children are wont to have towards their parents.

115. What is the sixth Mystery?

Honorable Marriage, which, in the first place, is made by the mutual consent and agreement of a man and woman (τοῦ ἀνδρὸς καὶ τῆς γυναικὸς) with each other, where there is no lawful impediment to hinder it.

However, this consent, alone, does not make the bond of marriage, unless the same persons do confirm their espousals, by a mutual testimony before a Priest, and by pledging their faith, each to the other, with joined hands, that they will be faithful to and honor each other; that they will love each other with all conjugal affection, in all states and conditions of life; and that they will never forsake each other, unto the end of their lives.

Secondly, This, their consent and promise, is confirmed and hallowed by the Priest; as it is written, Marriage is honorable in all, and the bed undefiled (Hebrews 13:4).

116. What are the fruits of this Mystery?

First, That a person might hereby be preserved from the danger of fornication and intemperance (τῆς πορνείας καὶ ἀκρασίας): For honorable Marriage was, therefore, instituted that the fire of lust might thereby be extinguished; according to St. Paul, To avoid fornication, let every man have his own wife, and let every woman have her own husband (1 Corinthians 7:2).

Secondly, That is deservedly to be honored, whereby the procreation of children is honestly provided for.

Thirdly, That in sickness, or any other distress, the husband might be to the wife, and the wife unto the husband, a faithful companion and helpmate, because of that especial affection and strict bond of love wherewith they are united. As testifies the Scripture, For this cause shall a man leave his father and mother, and shall hold fast unto his own wife, and they shall be one flesh (Genesis 2:24).

117. What is the seventh Mystery of the Church?

The Anointing of the Sick (εὐχέλαιον, literally prayer-oil). This was instituted by Christ himself, for when he sent out his Disciples two by two, They anointed with oil many that were sick, and healed them (Mark 6:13). The same was afterwards received by the universal Church, for a solemn custom, as appears from the Epistle of St. James:

If any be sick among you, let him call for the Elders of the Church, and let them pray over him, anointing him with oil, in the name of the Lord; and the prayer of faith shall save the sick, and the Lord shall raise him up; and if he has committed sins, they shall be forgiven him. (James 5:14-15)

118. What is to be observed in this Mystery?

First, That this Mystery, and all belonging unto it, be only performed by Priests, and none others.

Secondly, That the oil be pure, and not prepared with any mixtures; and that the sick person be an Orthodox Christian; and, also, that he have, beforehand, confessed all his sins unto his spiritual father.

Thirdly, That while the anointing is being administered, that prayer be repeated wherein the power and efficacy of this Mystery is expounded.

119. What are the fruits of this Mystery?

We are taught by St. James, in the place last mentioned (James 5:14-15), what are the fruits and benefits of this Mystery; namely, the forgiveness of sins, or the healing of the soul, and the restoring of health unto the body. And, however, although this latter is not always obtained, yet, nevertheless, the forgiveness of sins is always most assuredly thereby received.

c3FU

The Eleventh Article: Of the General Resurrection.

120. What is the Eleventh Article of the Faith?

I look for the Resurrection of the Dead.

121. What does this Article of the Faith teach?

It teaches the undoubted and most certain Resurrection (ἀνάστασις) of the human body (τῶν ἀνθρωπίνων σομάτων); of the good, and also of the wicked: according to the word of the Lord, saying:

All who are in the graves shall hear the voice of the Son of God, and shall come forth; they that have done good, unto the resurrection of life (ζωή); and they that have done evil, unto the resurrection of judgment (κρίσις). (John 5:28-29)

And they shall arise with the same bodies which they lived with before in this world; according to that of Job:

I know that my Redeemer lives, and that at the last he shall stand upon the earth; and though after my skin is destroyed, yet in my flesh shall I see God: Whom I shall see for myself, and mine eyes shall behold, and not another, though my within me my heart wither. (Job 19:25-27)

But this body (σῶμα), which will, verily, as we have said, be the selfsame one which was laid down in the grave — this body shall be raised, incorruptible and immortal; As says St. Paul:

We shall not all sleep, but we shall all be changed, in the twinkling of an eye, at the last trumpet; for the trumpet shall sound, and the dead shall be raised incorruptible, and we shall be changed. For this corruptible must put on incorruption, and this mortal must put on immortality. (1 Corinthians 15:51-53)

We are, moreover, to understand that the soul of every one shall return into its own body (σῶμα), and, together with it, shall receive the full and everlasting reward of its works.[131] The bodies of the wicked, also, shall be made immortal (ἀθάνατα), to receive eternal tormenting punishment (κολάζεσθαι αἰωνίως; Latin: aeternis discrucianda suppliciis).

122. What more does this Article teach?

It teaches every Christian to ever bear constantly in mind these four things — Death, the Last Judgment, the Torments of Hades, and, lastly, the everlasting Kingdom of Heaven.[132]

123. What benefit shall a man receive by being mindful of these four things?

Constant reflection on these things will beget devotion, watchfulness against sin, fear of God, fear of Hades’ torments (ᾅδην γεένης; Latin: geennae infernae; i.e. Hell’s fire), and love for Heaven’s Kingdom.

And whosoever duly considers these things will undoubtedly take care to prepare himself for the world to come: For, by thinking on that Last Day wherein the whole world shall be judged, he will be mindful to make himself ready for the account which he must then give of all his thoughts, words, and actions. The thought of Hades (ᾅδου; Latin: inferis) will quicken his endeavours to avoid it, and the consideration of Heaven’s Kingdom must excite his utmost earnestness to attain its joys.

c3GG

The Twelfth Article: Of the World to Come.

124. What is the Twelfth Article of the Faith?

And the life of the world to come.

125. What does the Church teach us in this Article of the Faith?

That in the world to come (μέλλοντα αἰῶνα), the grace and blessing of God shall be given unto the Elect; and life eternal, filled with joy and spiritual delight: As the holy Scripture testifies, Eye has not seen, nor ear heard, neither have entered into the heart of man, the things which God has prepared for them that love him (1 Corinthians 2:9). And again, to the same effect, The Kingdom of God is not food and drink, but righteousness, peace, and joy in the Holy Spirit (Romans 14:17).

126. Shall the soul only, or shall the body also, together with the soul, enjoy eternal happiness?

As the soul and body jointly did good works for the hope of eternal rewards, even so shall they jointly, and not separately, receive and enjoy the same triumphant happiness and felicity.

For the joy of the soul and of the body are not distinct and different things, but the same; and, therefore, as the body shall be glorified, so the whole man, both soul and body, shall be made glorious; and shall be made like the Angels, and equal unto them: According to the Scripture, which says, In the Resurrection they neither marry, nor are given in marriage, but are as the Angels of God in Heaven (Matthew 22:30).

The body shall be glorious, immortal, incorruptible, not needing food or drink; and the soul shall be the same: As says the Scripture:

The dead shall be raised, incorruptible, and we shall be changed. For this corruptible must put on incorruption, and this mortal must put on immortality. (1 Corinthians 15:52-53)

Now, this joint felicity will consist in contemplating the most blessed Trinity, and in bearing a delightful part in the spiritual harmony of the angelic chorus: According to the Apostle, Now we see as through a glass, darkly; but then face to face. Now I know in part; but then shall I know even as also I am known (1 Corinthians 13:12).

Now, whereas the Lord said unto Moses, There shall no man see my face and live (Exodus 33:20), this is to be understood of this present life, and of this corruptible body, not as yet glorified; and before that ultimate and most perfect redemption.

But after that complete glorification, in the future and eternal life, after the Day of Judgment, God will so clothe us with Light, that we shall be able to behold the Light of God: According to the Psalmist, With you is the Fountain of Life, and in your Light shall we see Light (Psalm 36:9). And this Light, as it shall be plainly and fully beheld, will entirely satisfy us, and gratify, to the utmost, all our desires of wisdom and beauty. For all good is contained in the contemplation and profession of the supreme Good; and the perfection of happiness, in the complete enjoyment of it: As the divine Psalmist declares, I shall appear in righteousness before your face: I shall be satisfied when your glory appears (Psalm 16:15 LXX).

c3H7

Part II: Of Hope.

127. What is hope?

Hope is a true confidence in God, bestowed on the heart of man by the divine enlightening and inspiration, that he might never despair of God’s grace and favor in granting his petitions, whether they be for pardon of his sins, or for any other spiritual or temporal good. Concerning which the Apostle says thus, Cast not away, therefore, your confidence, which has great reward (Hebrews 10:35). And elsewhere:

We are saved in this hope; but hope that is seen is not hope; for why does one still hope for what he sees? But if we hope for what we do not see, then we, through patience, wait eagerly for it. (Romans 8:24-25)

128. Whence have we this sure and certain hope?

All our hope is in the Lord Jesus Christ; as the Apostle says, By the commandment of God our Savior, and the Lord Jesus Christ our hope (1 Timothy 1:1). Because from him we receive all things: as Christ himself teaches, Whatsoever you shall ask in my Name, that will I do, that the Father may be glorified in the Son (John 14:13).

Now, hereby is to be understood the divine grace; for by Jesus Christ both grace and truth were given unto us: According to the blessed Scripture, For the Law was given by Moses, but grace and truth by Jesus Christ (John 1:17). And in this grace all our hope is founded.

We also place great confidence in the observation of God's Commands, seeing Christ has said:

He who has my commandments, and keeps them, it is he that loves me; and he that loves me shall be loved of my Father, and I will love him, and will manifest myself unto him. (John 14:21)

Moreover, our hope is confirmed by the communion of the dread and most holy Mysteries; namely, of the Body and Blood of Christ; whereby the Lord himself does dwell in us: As he himself testifies, He that eats my flesh and drinks my blood dwells in me, and I in him (John 6:56).

Lastly, our faith is established by constant prayer: As we are taught by the Apostle, Is any among you afflicted? Let him pray (James 5:13). And another Apostle says:

You, beloved, building yourselves up on your most holy faith, praying in the Holy Spirit, keep yourselves in the love of God, looking for the mercy of our Lord Jesus Christ unto eternal life. (Jude 20-21)

129. What is necessary to be considered in order to a right comprehension of this second part of the Orthodox Confession?

In this second part of the Orthodox Confession, it seems best that we should take into consideration the Lord's Prayer and the Nine beatitudes; for whatsoever we would obtain of God, we must ask both with a true and living faith, and also with hope that God will assuredly grant out petitions: According to the Apostle, Faithful is he who calls you; who also will do it (1 Thessalonians 5:24).

In like manner, through hope, we make the beatitudes ours when we exercise those virtues in the hope of obtaining the blessings which are promised as a reward for them.

Of Prayer.

130. What is Prayer?

Prayer is a certain petition offered up to God, out of an ardent faith, with the hope of obtaining that which we ask, according to his will.

Or it is thus to be defined: Prayer is a lifting up of our minds and wills to God, whereby we praise him or beseech him, or give thanks unto him for his benefits towards us.[133]

131. How ought a man to prepare himself for praying?

It is to be observed that there are three principal kinds of prayer.

First, When we give thanks to God for his benefits unto us, as the ancient people of Israel gave thanks unto God for their deliverance out of Egypt. In like manner, we ought, at all times, to give thanks for the great benefits we have received at his hands, but more especially for that he has delivered us from the bondage of the most cruel Enemy of our souls: According to the Apostle, Pray without ceasing; in everything give thanks; for this is the will of God in Christ Jesus concerning you (1 Thessalonians 5:17-18). And again, I thank my God always on your behalf for the grace of God, which is given you by Jesus Christ (1 Corinthians 1:4). And also elsewhere:

Giving thanks unto the Father, who has qualified us to be partakers of the inheritance of the saints in light; who has delivered us from the power of darkness, and has translated us into the Kingdom of his dear Son. (Colossians 1:12-13)

The second kind of prayer is that whereby we beseech God for pardon of our sins; that is, that he would deliver us from the punishment due to them, and that he would pour out upon our souls and bodies the abundance of his holy grace. Of this kind, we offer up our prayers both for ourselves and for our neighbor; according to the Apostle:

For this cause we also, since the day we heard it, do not cease to pray for you, and to desire that you might be filled with the knowledge of his will, in all wisdom and spiritual understanding. (Colossians 1:9)

The third kind of prayer is that wherein we praise and magnify the Lord our God for his excellent majesty and immortal glory; according to that of the holy Prophet David:

Every day will I give thanks unto you; and I will praise your Name for ever and ever. Great is the Lord, and marvellous, worthy to be praised; there is no end of his greatness. One generation shall praise your works unto another, and declare your power. (Psalm 145:2-4)

Of this kind, especially, is the Great Doxology (Glory to God in the highest, etc.) which we daily say or sing in the Church.

132. What is further necessary in order to pray?

It is requisite that when we go to pray we should rightly prepare ourselves; namely, with soberness, temperance, and reverence: As we are taught by the Apostle:

That, denying ungodliness and worldly lusts, we should live soberly, righteously, and godly in this present world. (Titus 2:12)

And also with compunction of heart, as the same Apostle says elsewhere:

Let the word of Christ dwell in you richly in all wisdom; teaching and admonishing one another in Psalms, and Hymns, and spiritual Songs; singing with grace in your hearts unto the Lord. (Colossians 3:16)

But all wrath and malice must be put away from him that goes to pray: According to the Scripture, If you forgive not men their trespasses, neither will your Father forgive your trespasses (Matthew 6:15). If, therefore, there be anyone that has anything against us, we must first be reconciled unto him: According to the Scripture:

Therefore, if you bring your gift to the altar, and there remember that your brother has something against you, leave your gift there before the altar, and go your way; first be reconciled to your brother, and then come and offer your gift. (Matthew 5:23-24)

We must also, when we are praying, put far away from us all other thoughts but those of devotion, that our prayer may be pure and acceptable to God; lest God pronounce of us as he did of the hypocrites, This people honors me with their lips, but their heart is far from me. But in vain do they worship me (Mark 7:6-7). And further, lest we be liable to that of the Psalmist, Let his prayer be turned into sin (Psalm 109:7).

c3JK

The Lord’s Prayer.

133. What is the Lord's Prayer?

Our Father, who art in Heaven;

Hallowed be Thy Name. 

Thy Kingdom come. 

Thy will be done on Earth, as it is in Heaven. 

Give us this day our daily bread, 

And forgive us our trespasses, as we forgive those who trespass against us. 

And lead us not into temptation, 

But deliver us from evil. 

For thine is the Kingdom, and the power, and the glory, forever, Amen.

134. Into how many Parts is the Lord's Prayer divided?

Three: (1) The Preface, or Invocation; (2) the Petitions; and (3) the Conclusion.

The Preface: God’s Heavenly Fatherhood.

135. What is the Preface, or Invocation?

Our Father, who art in Heaven.

136. What does this Invocation mean?

First, It teaches that whoso prays unto God ought to appear before him, not only as his creature, but also as his son by grace; for unless he be his son he cannot call upon God as his Father.

Now, this grace of adoption, whereby we are made the sons of God, we receive from Christ, who has bestowed it on those that believe in him: As says the Scripture, As many as received him, to them he gave power to become the sons of God (John 1:12). And elsewhere, Because you are sons, God has sent forth the Spirit of his Son into your hearts, crying, Abba, Father (Galatians 4:6). And for this reason, therefore, we call God our Father.

Secondly, It is requisite that he who goes about to pray should be a son of the catholic and Orthodox Church; for whosoever has not the Church for a Mother, neither can he have God for his Father: As it is said, Tell it unto the Church; but if he refuses to hear the Church, let him be to you as a Gentile and a tax-collector (Matthew 18:17).

Thirdly, Let not the devout petitioner doubt but that he shall receive that which he asks for, seeing he prays unto the most indulgent and merciful, as the common Father of all: According to the Scripture, Be merciful, just as your Father is merciful (Luke 6:36), who not only does not deny us, but even does himself stir us up to ask of him, and teaches us how; and with infinite mercy accepts our prayers, provided only they be offered up with humility and sincerity: For he well knows why we ask. And the Scripture says, Your Father knows of what you have need, before you ask him (Matthew 6:8). And in another place:

If you then, being evil, know how to give good gifts to your children, how much more shall your Father who is in Heaven give good things to them that ask him? (Matthew 7:11)

Fourthly, This Introduction, or Invocation, teaches us, that as God is the Father of us all, in like manner we, who are of the faithful, should be unto each other as brothers; and not only pray to God for ourselves, but also for our brethren, interceding for each other: According to the Scripture, Pray one for another, that you may be healed (James 5:16); namely, to God, who is our common Father; as Christ himself teaches us, Call no man your Father upon the Earth, for one is your Father which is in Heaven (Matthew 23:9). And when he beholds our brotherly love, which he so frequently recommends to us in his holy Gospel, he is both pleased with it, as a Father, and, also, more induced thereby to hear us when we pray, and to grant our petitions.

Fifthly, By these words, who art in Heaven, we are taught that when we pray we should raise our minds and thoughts wholly above all earthly and worldly things, and fix them entirely on Heaven and that which is incorruptible. Nevertheless, God, our Father, is not only in Heaven, but he is present in all places and fills all things: But whereas his grace and glory are more abundantly manifest in Heaven, it is, therefore, said to be his throne: According to that of the Psalmist, where he said, The Lord’s throne is in Heaven (Psalm 11:4), and also, The Lord has prepared his throne in Heaven, and his Kingdom rules over all (Psalm 103:19).

137. How many petitions are contained in the second part of this prayer of our Lord?

Seven.

The First Petition: God’s Hallowed Name.

138. What is the first petition in the Lord's Prayer?

Hallowed be thy Name.

139. What is contained in this petition?

First, We pray unto God that we may lead a life adorned with virtue and good works, that men may be excited to glorify the Name of God by the purity and holiness of our lives: According to that of St. Matthew, Let your light so shine before men that they may see your good works, and glorify your Father who is in Heaven (Matthew 5:16).

Secondly, We ask that not only our whole lives may be to the glory of God, but also that all those who do not know the true God, nor believe in him, may repent, and come to the knowledge of him, that the Name of God be glorified, both in them and by them.

We also beseech the Lord our God, in this petition, for those who bear indeed the name of Christians, but who live ungodly and scandalous lives, whereby our holy religion and God himself is blasphemed: Of whom the Apostle says, Having a form of godliness, but denying the power thereof (2 Timothy 3:5). And elsewhere, The Name of God is blasphemed among the Gentiles through you (Romans 2:24). For the Sake of these it is that, in this petition, God is besought; that such may turn from their wickedness, and bring forth good fruits, leading for the future a sober and godly life; and so the Name of God, by these means, also may be hallowed.

Moreover, we are to understand that although the Name of God is in itself most holy without our glorification, yet it is said to be hallowed by us, and in us, when by piety and holiness of life we sanctify ourselves to the glory of God's Name.

The Second Petition: God’s Kingdom Come.

140. What is the second petition?

Thy Kingdom come.

141. What is contained in this petition?

Herein we pray to God, first, that by his grace and justice and mercy he would reign in us altogether, especially in our hearts, and not suffer sin to have any dominion over us: As it is written, Let not sin, therefore, reign in your mortal body, that you should obey the lusts thereof (Romans 6:12).

Secondly, This petition expresses that a man who is in the state of grace with God, and feels the heavenly joy in his mind, cares not for this world, but is only solicitous and wishful to become an inhabitant in the heavenly Kingdom: As the Apostle, speaking of himself, says, I desire to depart and be with Christ (Philippians 1:23).

Thirdly, We beg of God that he would be pleased to hasten his Second Coming, wherein the Son of Man shall appear in his glory (Matthew 25:31), and the resurrection of the dead, and the Day of Judgment, by which the kingdom of this world and the Enemy of our souls may be taken away: and the Kingdom of Heaven come, that God may be all in all; as the Apostle speaks in 1 Corinthians 15:28.

The Third Petition: God’s Will Done.

142. What is the third petition?

Thy will he done on earth, as it is in Heaven.

143. What is contained in this third petition?

First, We beseech God that he would not suffer us to live in this world after our own wills, but that he would guide and govern us by his will and pleasure.

Secondly, We pray that neither we nor others might resist or dislike the will of God, but that, as the Angels in Heaven submit unto and obey the divine will in all things, without any delay or refusal, so, likewise, on earth, that all men may be obedient unto God without murmuring, and with all readiness and thanksgiving.

Thirdly, In this petition we express our belief that nothing can affect us, God's elect, without the divine will and permission, whether it be in relation to the holiness and innocence with which we ought to pass our lives, or to persecutions and temptations of the Enemy. Insomuch that the Lord not only takes care of our persons, but of even the very hairs of our head: According to the Scripture, Even the hairs of your head are all numbered (Luke 12:7). And again, Not a hair of your head shall perish (Luke 21:18).

The Fourth Petition: God’s Giving.

144. What is the fourth petition?

Give us this day our daily bread.

145. What is contained in this petition?

First, That divine and most excellent food of our souls, the Word of God, is here meant; concerning which the Scripture thus teaches us, Man shall not live by bread alone, but by every word that proceeds out of the mouth of God (Matthew 4:4). We therefore ask of God that he would keep us from want and famine of his holy Word; that is, the doctrine of Christ, without which doctrine our inward man would surely die, as if it were perished of hunger. Therefore we very properly, in this petition, remember that death of the soul which befalls those who refuse or neglect to hear the Word of God and divine instruction, and thereby set the bad example of an ill life.

Secondly, Herein is included that other food of our souls, namely, the Communion of the Body and Blood of Christ; for so the Lord has spoken concerning it:

My flesh is food indeed, and my blood is drink indeed: whosoever eats my flesh and drinks my blood, he abides in me, and I in him. (John 6:55-56)

Wherefore, that we may worthily partake of this food, we do, in this petition, beseech God that, out of his mercy and favor to mankind, he would be pleased to bestow himself upon us. And when we have these two kinds of food, then we have the Kingdom of God within us (Luke 17:21); and then God, of his own accord, will give unto us all such temporal and worldly things as the weakness and poverty of our mortal condition make necessary for us: As it is written, Seek first the Kingdom of God, and his righteousness, and all these things shall be added unto you (Matthew 6:33).

Thirdly, Under the name of bread, in this petition, are comprehended all things which are necessary for us in this present life, both with regard to our substance and our preservation.

However, let every one remember that superfluities and luxury are not hereby prayed for, but only a just and proper sufficiency; for those things which minister unto our voluptuousness, and are the instruments of sensuality, are no other than the seeds of misery and punishment. Whereof the Apostle, St. Paul, thus speaks, Let us walk honestly as in the day, not in carousing and drunkenness, not in lewdness and lust (Romans 13:13). And elsewhere, Having food and clothing, let us be content with these (1 Timothy 6:8).

Now, by the words this day is to be understood this present life, while we continue in this world; for in that which is to come we shall enjoy the very presence and sight of God, and the happiness arising therefrom.

The Fifth Petition: God’s Forgiveness.

146. What is the fifth petition?

And forgive us our trespasses, as we forgive those that trespass against us.

147. What is contained in this petition?

First, We do, in this petition, ask of God that he would forgive us our sins, especially those which we have committed since our Baptism; as well mortal sins as all others whereby we have offended God and our neighbor; by thought only or by a full design and intention of sin, or by words, or by actions.

Secondly, Whereas in this petition we say, Forgive us our trespasses, as we forgive those that trespass against us, now we do hereby engage our faith that we will also forgive our enemies. He, therefore, who does not forgive his neighbor, whatsoever he has committed against him, let him not use this petition; for he may not hope to have his sins forgiven. Nay, further, his very prayer will become a sin, according to the Psalmist, Let his prayer be turned into sin (Psalm 109:7). And truly with justice, for if we will not forgive our brethren their trifling offences against us, how can it be expected that God should forgive our sins against him, which will bear no comparison to the trespasses which we resent to our brethren; seeing we are daily, nay hourly, giving just cause for God's anger against us?

The Sixth Petition: God’s Leading.

148. What is the sixth petition?

And lead us not into temptation.

149. What is contained in this petition?

First, We pray to God that we may be free from all temptation; but if not from all, at least from those which exceed our strength.

Of these, some arise from the world, some from the Devil, and some from our own flesh, and entice us to sin. Again, others are inflicted by tyrants who solicit and harass the Church of God by erroneous doctrines, by deceitful flatteries and falsehood, by pretended miracles, by the allurements of promised riches and honors; in fine, by tyrannical force and persecutions; by open violence and robbery, and despoiling us of our possessions, and by loading us with shame and reproach. All which we woefully see in these our days.

Secondly, We beseech God, in this petition, that if we be called to pour out our blood, or to lay down our lives, for his most holy Name's sake, for the holy Church, which is his Spouse, and for the truth of the Gospel: That then he would be pleased to strengthen us, and support us with his grace, whereby we might be enabled to endure and overcome the most unusual or excessive torments with constancy and resolution, and obtain the crown of martyrdom in Heaven; and that he would be pleased not to let us suffer things greater than we can bear.

The Seventh Petition: God’s Delivering.

150. What is the seventh petition?

But deliver us from evil.

151. What is contained in this petition?

First, We herein pray to God to deliver us from all kinds of evil, especially from sin, and from all wickedness that provokes the wrath of God.

Secondly, That his grace might accompany us, and keep us from falling into his wrath, That he would not rebuke us in his anger (θυμῷ), neither chasten us in his wrath (ὀργῇ) (Psalm 6:1 LXX), for our sins; but that we might, Come before his presence with thanksgiving, and show ourselves glad in him with psalms (Psalm 95:2).

Fourthly, In this petition we also pray that we may be delivered from all sorts of evil and mischiefs whatsoever that are hard and difficult to be borne by the children of men: Such as famine, pestilence, war, fire, and such like. All which we hereby beseech God to turn away from us, and to grant us his merciful loving-kindness.

Fifthly, More especially we pray that in the hour of death he would protect us from all insults of the Enemy of our souls, and that he would give us grace to fight piously and securely under the protection of his favor, and the guide and safeguard of his Angels. For whosoever departs out of life in such a manner, that person is undoubtedly happy. It therefore behoves us, in the highest degree, that we should all most earnestly ask of God that we may be defended from the temptations and assaults of the Devil in our last moments.

Lastly, We pray, in this petition, that we may be delivered from the Devil and torments of Hell.

The Conclusion: God’s Sovereign Glory.

152. What is the third part of the Lord's Prayer?

This Conclusion: For thine is the Kingdom, and the power, and the glory, forever; Amen.

153. What is contained in this part, the Conclusion?

This Conclusion is twofold, in the first part corresponding to the Invocation. For as that makes us hope that we shall certainly obtain what we ask of God as we ought, since he is our Father of whom we ask it; so this Conclusion teaches to expect that our proper petition will be granted, because the whole universe belongs to our Father. He is the King of the whole world. To him all created things are subject. His is the power, and the glory, too, is his; which power and glory nothing can resist, whether in heaven or on earth. He, therefore, can assuredly give us all things, whatsoever we ask with faith and hope; for this cause only, the eternal glory of his Name: As it is expressed in these words, And the glory, for ever.

The second part of this Conclusion is the word Amen; in which word we pray that whatsoever we have asked may be granted unto us according to our desire: As the Apostle says:

This is the confidence that we have in him, that if we ask anything according to his will, he hears us. And if we know that he hears us, whatsoever we ask, we know that we have the petition that we desired of him. (1 John 5:14-15)

154. Is this Conclusion united to the Lord's Prayer?

It is certain that Christ himself, concluding his prayer, which he taught us, ends with these words; as appears from St. Matthew’s Gospel (Matthew 6:13).[134]

And the sense of them shows plainly that they are no ways inconsistent with the Lord's Prayer; rather that the prayer is greatly strengthened and perfected by them, seeing it is that God we call upon, whose power extends over the whole universe, and unto whom all things are subject.

And although lay persons do not use this conclusion, that hinders not, but that in the public prayers of the Church, and in private devotions, where a Priest is present, he should use them for adding the greater weight unto his prayer. Neither will it be amiss for a lay person to use them, if no Priest be present, any more than it would for him to recite any other part of the Gospel.

And it were better never to separate these words from the Lord's Prayer, it being evident that the Church orders them to be pronounced by the Priest only in the public divine services, only for the sake of adding the greater solemnity to this devotion.

c3P2

The Beatitudes.

155. Forasmuch as the beatitudes do very much establish and confirm our faith, I pray, what are they?

The beatitudes, which our Lord Christ has taught us in the fifth chapter of St. Matthew’s Gospel, are nine in number; on which St. Chrysostom thus comments, Moses delivered Ten Commandments, but Jesus, the Lord of Moses, gave Nine beatitudes.[135] And again, The Law gave unto us the Ten Commandments, but Jesus the Nine beatitudes, forming thereby a thrice threefold crown.

The First Beatitude: Poor in Spirit.

156. What is the first beatitude?

Blessed are the poor in spirit, for theirs is the Kingdom of Heaven.

157. What doctrine does this beatitude contain?

It relates to riches and the good things of this world; which, if it please God to bestow them on us, we should use, not as if they were our own, but as only being the stewards and dispensers of them, and not give ourselves up to great anxiousness or to covetousness: As we are admonished by the holy Psalmist, If riches increase, set not your heart upon them (Psalm 62:10).

We ought to imitate the ancient Christian perfection, and have nothing distinct, but all things in common: After the example of those Christians who lived in the infancy of the Church, of whom the Scripture testifies:

The multitude of them that believed were of one heart and of one soul; and no one said that any of the things which he possessed was his own, but they had all things in common. (Acts 4:32)

And after:

Nor were there any among them that lacked; for as many as were possessors of lands or houses sold them, and brought the proceeds of the things that were sold, and laid them down at the Apostles’ feet; and distribution was made unto every man according as he had need. (Acts 4:34-35)

And again, All that believed were together, and had all things in common (Acts 2:44).

It is this virtue that is called poverty of spirit. However, it is requisite and allowable that everyone retain to himself so much as is necessary for his due and proper support and accommodation, without luxury and extravagance.

In this virtue the monks and religious are deservedly allowed to excel, seeing they are not to be careful for anything as their own, not even for necessary food and clothing; but they suffer the want of all things with patience, in hopes of greater rewards in Heaven: Having before their eyes the words of the Apostle:

Even unto this present hour we both hunger and thirst, and are naked, and are buffeted, and have no certain dwelling-place; and labor, working with our own hands: being reviled, we bless; being persecuted, we suffer it; being defamed, we entreat; we are made as the filth of the world, the offscouring of all things, even unto now. (1 Corinthians 4:11-13)

For sufferings like these Christ offers the Kingdom of Heaven as a reward.

Nevertheless, such as do possess wealth, gained innocently and with justice, will also be partakers of salvation and eternal life, provided they possess their riches in righteousness; namely, if they use them for the relief of the necessities of the Church, in charitable giving to the poor, the stranger, the sick, and whosoever is in need. In like manner as Zacchceus did unto Christ himself when he spoke unto him thus:

Behold, Lord, the half of my goods I give to the poor, and if I have taken any thing from any man by false accusation, I restore him fourfold. (Luke 19:8)

But excessive spenders, who by overindulgence and extravagance are reduced to extreme poverty, are in no sort the object of this beatitude, such poverty being rather the proper punishment of their prodigality and unjust abuse of what they had. Nevertheless, they are not to be rejected from the charitable giving of good men, and if they reform and practise the virtues of poverty, they will also receive the reward of this beatitude from God.

The Second Beatitude: Mourning Sin.

158. What is the second beatitude? 

Blessed are those who mourn, for they shall be comforted.

159. What does this beatitude teach?

First, It teaches that those of the faithful are blessed who through their whole lives mourn and weep for their old sins and offences, whereby they have offended God and their neighbor; according to the words of the Prophet Jeremiah:

The children of Israel shall come, they and the children of Judah shall come together: With continual weeping they shall come and seek the Lord their God (Jeremiah 50:4).

But they have no part in this beatitude who mourn and weep only because of some worldly grief or misfortune. Nor they who, when they are condemned to suffer the just punishment due to their crimes, weep and mourn; not because they have sinned, but because of the fear of their just punishments which they must undergo for them. Nor they, likewise, who are concerned only on account of having lost some perishable thing or other, and the like.

Secondly, This beatitude teaches us that those are blessed who, with unfeigned sorrow, and tears shed for the sins of others, appease the wrath of God towards them, and pour forth their prayers before him that he would vouchsafe unto them the grace to repent of their sins: Namely, that he would bring back heretics to the Communion of the Church, and that he would convert the wicked from their evil and irregular lives to a sober and orderly course of life.

Thirdly, By this beatitude we are taught that those are blessed who are oppressed by the great and powerful ones of the world, and unjustly despoiled of their goods: Which sufferings they may not endeavour to revenge, but with pain of heart and mourning, pray to God for deliverance and safety, placing all their hope and confidence in God’s mercy, which they shall receive together with abundance of consolation, being therein rightly and truly partakers of the divine grace.

Those also are included in this beatitude who are afflicted for the Orthodox Faith and the sake of the Church: Such are all martyrs and the like.

The Third Beatitude: Meek.

160. What is the third beatitude?

Blessed are the meek, for they shall inherit the earth.

161. What does this beatitude teach?

This beatitude teaches us, First, That we should yield a ready and sincere obedience, with this virtue of meekness and humility, to God and the Church of Christ, and also to our superiors, following the example of Jesus Christ, our most meek Lord. And when we give this obedience to our superiors, we should propose within ourselves that it is to Christ, and for his sake. So we should also refer to Christ the respect and reverence which we show to his Priests, our spiritual pastors.

Secondly, This beatitude teaches us that they are blessed who are not grievous or troublesome to any man; who do not rob people of their good name, and are not criticizers and condemners of others; but esteeming themselves as the lowest, are always ready to condemn their own life and actions, and think of their own good deeds as unclean and unworthy.

However, if those whose duty it is to reprove others should, through the warmth of their zeal, use some harsh expressions in correcting a sinner, not out of contempt or spitefulness, but for the good of his spiritual welfare, such a one is not, however, exempted from this beatitude, seeing he does that which is his duty: As the Apostle teaches:

Brethren, if a man be overtaken in a fault, you which are spiritual restore such a one in the spirit of meekness; watching yourself, lest you also be tempted. (Galatians 6:1)

But if anyone says that it is not possible for a mortal man thus to subdue his anger, let such a one know that he ought not pour out his wrath and anger on his neighbor, but on the Devil, who is the tempter and instigator of men to all kinds of evil.

Those, therefore, who are endued with this virtue shall receive the promised inheritance of the earth, an abundance of God's goodness in this life, and eternal happiness in that which is to come: According to the Scripture, which says, I believe that I shall see the goodness of the Lord in the land of the living (Psalm 27:13).

The Fourth Beatitude: Hungry for Righteousness.

162. What is the fourth beatitude?

Blessed are those who hunger and thirst after righteousness, for they shall be filled.

163. What does this beatitude teach?

It teaches us that they are blessed who, having sustained an injury, cannot obtain redress from justice, either because of their religion or their poverty; or for want of health, or because they are strangers; or for that their adversary is too powerful, or through any other unjust cause.

Wherefore those who are set in the seat of justice, and whose office it is to judge, ought to be most conscientious and careful that the poor, the widow, and the orphan be not oppressed with unrighteous judgment, remembering what the Scripture says unto them, Learn to do well, seek judgment, relieve the oppressed, judge the fatherless, plead for the widow (Isaiah 1:17). Which duties if they neglect, then those who suffer oppression are said to hunger and thirst after justice or righteousness, and they are the objects of this beatitude. And unto these unjust judges God will render severe punishment: According to the Scripture:

Lord, you have heard the desire of the poor; you, prepare their heart, and your ear hearkens thereunto. To give justice to the fatherless and poor, that the man of the earth be no more exalted against them. (Psalm 10:17-18)

The Fifth Beatitude: Merciful.

164. What is the fifth beatitude?

Blessed are the merciful, for they shall receive mercy.

165. What does this beatitude teach us?

That they are blessed who discharge the duties of mercy.

The Works of Mercy.

166. What are the works of mercy?

The works of mercy are twofold, some regarding the soul, and others relating to the body (Blessed Theophylact, Commentary on Matthew, chapter 25).

Seven of the Body.
