Eternal Memory (3)

HERE IS MADE THE ACCLAMATION OF THE KINGS, PATRIARCHS, AND ALL THE LIVING. THE HOLY TRINITY HATH GLORIFIED THEM.

Let us beseech God that we be instructed and strengthened by their conflicts and struggles unto death for the sake of piety and by their teachings, and let us fervently pray to be shown forth unto the end as imitators of their godly conversation. May we be deemed worthy of the fulfillment of our petitions by the compassions and grace of the great and first High Priest Christ, our true God, by the intercessions of our most glorious Lady Theotokos and Ever-Virgin Mary, of the Godlike Angels, and of all the Saints. Amen.

FROM THE COUNCIL OF 1583

From old Rome have come certain persons who learned there to wear Latin habits. The worst of it is how, from being Romans of Rumelia bred and born, they not only have changed their faith, but they even wage war upon the Orthodox dogmas and truths of the Eastern Church which have been delivered to us by Christ and the divine Apostles and the Holy Councils (or Synods) of the Holy Fathers. Therefore, cutting off these persons as rotten members, we command:

That whoever does not confess with heart and mouth that he is a child of the Eastern Church baptized in Orthodox style, and that the Holy Spirit proceeds out of only the Father, essentially and hypostatically, as Christ says in the Gospel, shall be outside of our Church and shall be anathematized. That whoever does not confess that at the Mystery of Holy Communion the laity must also partake of both kinds, of the Precious Body and Blood, but instead says that he will partake only of the body, and that this is sufficient because therein is both flesh and blood, when as a matter of fact Christ said and administered each separately, and they who fail to keep such customs, let all such persons be anathematized.

That whoever says that our Lord Jesus Christ at the Mystic Supper had unleavened bread (made without yeast), like that of the Jews, and not leavened bread, that is to say, bread raised with yeast, let him depart far away from us and let him be anathema as one having Jewish views and those of Apolinarios and bringing dogmas of the Armenians into our Church, on which account let him be doubly anathema.

Whoever says that our Christ and God, when He comes to judge us, does not come to judge souls together with bodies, or embodied souls, but instead comes to sentence only bodies, let him be anathema.

Whoever says that the souls of Christians who repented while in the world but failed to perform their penance go to a purgatory of fire when they die, where there is flame and punishment, and are purified, which is simply an ancient Greek myth, and those who, like Origen, think that hell is not everlasting, and thereby afford or offer the liberty or incentive to sin, let him and all such persons be anathema.

That whoever says that the Pope is the head of the Church, and not Christ, and that he has authority to admit persons to Paradise with his letters of indulgence or other passports, and can forgive sins as many as a person may commit if such person pay money to receive from him indulgences, i.e., licenses to sin, let every such person be anathema.

That whoever does not follow the customs of the Church as the Seven Holy Ecumenical Councils decreed, and Holy Pascha, and the Menologion with which they did well in making it a law that we should follow it, and wishes to follow the newly-invented Paschalion and the New Menologion of the atheist astronomers of the Pope, and opposes all those things and wishes to overthrow and destroy the dogmas and customs of the Church which have been handed down by our fathers, let him suffer anathema and be put out of the Church of Christ and out of the Congregation of the Faithful.

That you pious and Orthodox Christians remain faithful in what you have been taught and have been born and brought up in, and when the time calls for it and there be need, that your very blood be shed in order to safeguard the Faith handed down by our Fathers and your confession: and that ye beware of such persons as have been described or referred to in the foregoing paragraphs, in order that our Lord Jesus Christ may help you and at the same time may the prayer of our mediocrity be with all of you: Amen.

Done in the year of the God-man 1583, year of indiction 12, November 20,

Jeremiah of Constantinople

Silvester of Alexandria

Sophronius of Jerusalem

In the presence of the rest of the prelates at the Council.
[1] On Predestination, also see Question 30 in St. Peter Mogila’s Catechism.[2] Unrepented sin is the sole cause of condemnation.[3] Not in the sense of learning or discovering, but knowing fundamentally.[4] He knew from outside of time, not as a consequence of forseen temporal sequence (as this would contradict omniscience). This is to say, God foreknew according to each person’s unique, total persona or identity who accepts grace and who rejects it, before they come to exist, and before they make any decision. He then predestined some, on the basis of the exhaustively known persona, prior to and not on the basis of any foreseen work (as this would reverse causation and make man the author of his own salvation, which Dositheus below denies). As St. John Chrysostom teaches in Homily 16, on Romans 9:11-13: “He does not wait, as man does, to see from the issue of their acts the good and him who is not so, but even before these He knows which is the wicked and which not such.” The cause of predestination, therefore, is God’s grace, in time freely received; the cause of condemnation is man’s sin, in time freely willed.[5] Predestination is the fact of salvation prior to its happening in time.[6] In other words, the cause of condemnation is not in God, but in man’s free rejection of God’s grace.[7] Or prevenient, i.e. preceding or preliminary.[8] i.e. unconditionally.[9] Preceding grace illumines; particular grace empowers.[10] In other words, God’s grace precedes, empowers, and accomplishes man’s salvation through the medium of man’s consenting and co-operating free will: “For it is God who works (ἐνεργέω) in you both to will (θέλω) and to work (ἐνεργέω) for His good pleasure” (Philippians 2:13). Man’s consent is not a cause of grace, but a response to grace. Dositheus is confessing that particular grace follows upon man’s response to preceding or prevenient grace. cf. Blessed Augustine, On Grace and Free Will, ch. 21.[11] There is a disjunct between will and ability, as St. Paul illustrates: “For what I will to do, that I do not practice; but what I hate, that I do. ... For the good that I will to do, I do not do; but the evil I will not to do, that I practice” (Romans 7:15, 19). In other words, willingness can be present without ability. Preceding grace makes willing the spiritual good possible, while particular grace makes the ability for spiritual good possible. Without preceding grace, it is impossible to will the spiritually good; without particular grace it is impossible to do the spiritually good. As hell is the removal of God’s grace, repentance is rendered impossible for the condemned, as both the grace to will and the grace to do are removed. Freedom of will is retained, naturally, but moves exclusively within a narrow set of freely chosen evil desires.[12] In other words, although works are not a cause of salvation, their presence is a necessary concomitant to faith such that, to save, faith cannot be alone. Salvation thus occurs necessarily with works, and condemnation with sin.[13] Not independently as a per se cause of salvation, but as a necessary concomitant and corollary of right faith, i.e. living faith (James 2:14-26), such that through one’s grace-empowered works of faith salvation is consequently had. See also Decree 13, which expressly denies that saving faith can be alone.[14] See Hebrews 5: “And having been perfected, He [Christ] became the author of eternal salvation to all who obey Him” (Hebrews 5:9). And Hebrews 12: “Looking unto Jesus, the author and finisher of our faith” (Hebrews 12:2).[15] i.e. unconditionally.[16] This serves to reject both cheap grace and soteriological fatalism. Why any one person rejects this grace is a mystery. See St. John of Damascus, Exposition of the Faith, Book 4, chapter 21.[17] Contra theological fatalism, which holds that man cannot choose meaningfully between good and evil, that all choices are given, and that in salvation man’s will is bypassed and/or coerced.[18] God categorically does not cause a person to damningly reject Him, nor withhold that which could save a person were they consenting, nor coerce consent.[19] St. Cyril of Alexandria, in Sermon 42 on the Gospel of St. Luke (8:19-21), places man’s man’s fall in light of Christ’s redemption: “It was the Only-begotten Word of God, by submitting Himself to our estate, and being found in fashion as a man, and becoming obedient unto the Father even unto death. Thus has the guilt of the disobedience that is by Adam been remitted: thus has the power of the curse ceased, and the dominion of death been brought to decay. And this too Paul teaches, saying, ‘For as by the disobedience of the one man, the many became sinners, so by the obedience of the One, the many became righteous’ (Romans 5:19). For the whole nature of man became guilty in the person of him who was first formed; but now it is wholly justified again in Christ. For He became for us the second commencement of our race after that primary one; and therefore all things in Him have become new.”[20] i.e. particular sins, as the list following will show. In other words, man is born having a condition of sinfulness, as per fallen nature, not born having particular acts of sin, what is sometimes referred to as inherited or original guilt.[21] Despite receiving in his nature the ancestral sin, man has not lost his original nature, nor the image of God in which he was made, and is not compelled to sin.[22] See the following footnote. For as is the case with the Theotokos, St. John the Forerunner is also affirmed, though bearing the ancestral sin, to be without personal sin.[23] cf. the Council of Ephesus, July 22nd Session. cf. Athanasius, Against the Arians, Discourse 3, paragraph 33, which the Ephesus Council cites. The same is quoted in Session 1 of the Council of Chalcedon.[24] This denies the erroneous doctrines of the Immaculate Conception and bodily Assumption as held by the Roman Catholics.[25] St. Cyril of Alexandria, in his third sermon on the Gospel of St. Luke (2:21-24), comments: “Christ therefore ransomed from the curse of the law those who being subject to it, had been unable to keep its enactments. And in what way did He ransom them? By fulfilling it. And to put it in another way: in order that He might expiate the guilt of Adam’s transgression, He showed Himself obedient and submissive in every respect to God the Father in our stead: for it is written, ‘That as through the disobedience of the One man, the many were made sinners, so also through the obedience of the One, the many shall be made just’ (Romans 5:19). He yielded therefore His neck to the law in company with us, because the plan of salvation so required: for it became Him to fulfil all righteousness. For having assumed the form of a slave, as being now enrolled by reason of His human nature among those subject to the yoke, He once even paid the half shekel to the collectors of the tribute, although by nature free, and as the Son not liable to pay the tax. When therefore them seest Him keeping the law, be not offended, nor place the free-born among the slaves, but reflect rather upon the profoundness of the plan of salvation.”[26] The term mediator is thus understood in at least two senses, one being in an ultimate soteriological sense, applying to Christ alone, the other in an instrumental sense, a sense which can apply to the intercession of saints on earth and in heaven, as well as to Angels.[27] Observance of the commandments is the substance of faith working in love.[28] Note that the principle of works is not defined as an independent principle from faith, i.e. faith plus works, but as of one single principle with faith, which extends from faith, through faith, and by which faith is exercised and made savingly manifest. Justification is thus not by works, but by faith that works.[29] St. Cyril of Alexandria, in his 40th Sermon on Luke (7:36-50), demonstrates the unity of grace, faith, and works: “Christ’s gifts therefore raise men to a hope long looked for, and to a most dear joy. The woman who was guilty of many impurities, and deserving of blame for most disgraceful deeds, was justified, that we also may have confidence that Christ certainly will have mercy upon us, when He sees us hastening to Him, and endeavouring to escape from the pitfalls of wickedness. Let us too stand before Him: let us shed the tears of repentance: let us anoint Him with ointment: for the tears of him that repenteth are a sweet savour to God. Call him to mind who saith, ‘Awake, they who are drunken with wine: weep and howl all they who drink wine to drunkenness.’ For Satan intoxicates the heart, and agitates the mind by wicked pleasure, leading men down to the pollutions of sensuality. But while there is time, let us awake; and as most wise Paul says, ‘Let us not be constantly engaged in revels and drunkenness, nor in chambering and wantonness; but rather let us work what is good: for we are not of the night, nor of darkness, but children of light and of the day. Let us therefore cast off the works of darkness, and clothe ourselves with the works of light.’ Be not troubled when thou meditatest upon the greatness of thy former sins: but rather know, that still greater is the grace that justifieth the sinner, and absolveth the wicked. Faith then in Christ is found to be the pledge to us of these great blessings: for it is the way that leadeth unto life... that makes us members of the kingdom of Christ.”[30] i.e. heaven.[31] μετέξει πληρεστάτῃ ἐνεργείας τοῦ τελεταρχικοῦ πνεύματος[32] Apostolic succession and continuity include at least three aspects: apostolic doctrine, episcopal ordination, and diachronic koinonia, which is to say that each generation would recognize the other as being in communion.[33] Canon 1 of the Apostolic Canons reads: “Let a bishop be ordained by two or three bishops.”[34] i.e. Chrismation.[35] Note carefully that the teaching is not faith plus works, as if works were a kind of second principle extrinsically coordinated with faith, but faith which itself works through love. Such works are thus works of faith.[36] ἔξωθεν, the sense specified in the Synodical Letter to the Synod at Jassy, critique 9.[37] i.e. as a transaction following upon bare assent, i.e. a mere transfer of labels, from sinful righteous, divorced from actual transformation.[38] A righteousness that is merely extrinsic or imputed undermines the reality of metanoia, essential to salvation, which is the fundamental transformation of the person. Impartation, which is an intrinsic communication of grace that is cooperated with, received by faith, is also necessary; for the reality of Christ’s righteousness must extend into and through the person: faith working in love.[39] This condemns bare faith and cheap grace, or what some call “free grace.” Bare faith is barren faith, a chimera, which is to say no faith at all.[40] Universalism (apokatastasis) is emphatically denied.[41] Τοὐναντίον δὲ μᾶλλον πιστεύομεν, ὅτι οὐ τῆς πίστεως ἀναφορικὸν ἀλλά τὴν οὖσαν ἐν ἡμῖν πίστιν διὰ τῶν ἔργων δικαιοῦν ἡμᾶς παρά Χριστοῦ.[42] This responds to Lucaris who, in the 13th chapter, defines faith’s referent (ἀναφορικὸν) as “the righteousness of Christ.”[43] i.e. the faith that is in us that actually cooperates with the grace of Christ. A merely forensic or extrinsic (ἔξωθεν) justification is insufficient for salvation. Faith, which receives Christ’s righteousness, and certainly has a forensic aspect, justifies only when it is living, actual, i.e. when it is faith working through love. This is the twin reality of imputation and impartation, which avoids two ditches: on the one side, cheap grace, and on the other, self-justification (Pelagianism).[44] In other words, the reality of faith, and so of justification, is not in mere words or forensic declarations. Only the kind of faith that cooperates with grace, i.e. works through love, receives Christ’s righteousness, and is thus justified, because Christ’s righteousness always comes with power, not mere assertions, therefore one is only truly justified by the kind of faith that energizes works of love. Forensic (extrinsic) justification rightly applies only in these conditions.[45] Not justifying ourselves, we are justified by Christ when we have faith working through love. This emphasizes that the internalization of grace through faith is made active through works, thus justifying a person. This denies both that one is saved through bare assent, i.e. “faith simply alone,” and that one saves himself through his own works with Christ merely assisting (of which see Decree 14).[46] This would again make works a kind of second principle. It would also lead the soul to look to its own works for salvation rather than to Christ.[47] i.e. fruits of faith, which is to say direct products of living faith.[48] Note that the faith is what is efficacious, i.e. justifying. The faith that works in love is not faith + works + love = 3 things that are needed for salvation, but a single thing, faith, that works (in love). Thus we are justified by faith that works, i.e. faith and works, not faith plus works, for we show a single thing, faith, by our works (James 2:18).[49] Not that works merit salvation, but varying weights of glory.[50] Man, being fallen, does not cease having the natural powers proper to man.[51] i.e. man retains the natural power to choose good and reject evil. Retaining this, it is yet affirmed in Chapter 1 of the Synod of Jerusalem that man regards “as natural what is against nature,” and that “he is inclined towards evil.” This excludes the possibility of Pelagianism.[52] The power to will is a property of human nature, and so if a person were by nature unable to will the good, then it would mean human nature is not merely fallen or corrupt, but evil. Thus evil choice would be the natural product of an evil nature, making evil part of nature, which is both absurd and blasphemous. For not only does evil not have a nature, but God being the author of nature He cannot be the author of an evil nature or of sin. As Decree 4 states: “For it is a true and infallible rule, that God is in no way the author of evil, nor can it at all by just reasoning be attributed to God.”[53] Dositheus is distinguishing between natural good and spiritual good.[54] Works without faith do not justify, nor do they save, nor do they damn.[55] It is works of faith, wrought by grace and with grace, in the regenerated (regeneration also being by grace), that renders a person worthy. Therefore, salvation is received by grace through faith working in love. Worthiness is thus not the sum of natural perfection but of supernatural grace working in and through man, man freely cooperating with God’s saving energy.[56] This has also been called civil or social righteousness.[57] Not in the sense of adding to Christ’s salvation, or of aiding Christ, but in the sense of actively and willingly receiving salvation.[58] This points to a profound mystery as regards God being the Lord of the natural and the supernatural, wherein the Sacraments he binds the natural and the supernatural together in order to provide a tangible means of grace.[59] “The devil, in bringing about our death in a twofold sense [i.e. bodily and spiritually]... we for our compliance were condemned” (Synod of Jerusalem, Against the Third Question, pg 52).[60] This reference could not be confirmed.[61] On Baptism, Against the Donatists, Book 4, chapter 24, cf. chapter 23.[62] This and the following reference could not be confirmed. Bl. Augustine, however, wrote On Baptism, Against the Donatists, in six books, and also in three books: On the Merits and Forgiveness of Sins, and on the Baptism of Infants.[63] Blessed Augustine taught, in Book 4, Chapter 25, On Baptism: “The sacrament of baptism is one thing, the conversion of the heart another; but that man’s salvation is made complete through the two together. Nor are we to suppose that, if one of these be wanting, it necessarily follows that the other is wanting also; because the sacrament may exist in the infant without the conversion of the heart; and this was found to be possible without the sacrament in the case of the thief, God in either case filling up what was involuntarily wanting. But when either of these requisites is wanting intentionally, then the man is responsible for the omission. And baptism may exist when the conversion of the heart is wanting; but, with respect to such conversion, it may indeed be found when baptism has not been received, but never when it has been despised. Nor can there be said in any way to be a turning of the heart to God when the sacrament of God is treated with contempt.”[64] Notice the reference to justification (δικαιοῦν), which relates to Decree 13.[65] This is also called baptismal regeneration, where the new life received is that of Christ.[66] This is against the idea that sins are merely described as forgiven, as if it was a legal reality without sin’s energy and power also being washed away. God does declare them as forgiven, i.e. they are no longer imputed, but in doing so He also truly imparts the energy of grace to actually remove sin.[67] i.e. the Lord’s Supper, the very one He instituted, the one He shared with His disciples, and the Eucharist we partake of, are one with the Cross, and so one Sacrifice, one Supper, not many suppers, not many sacrifices, nor repetitions. cf. Chapter 4 of the Synodical documents, which declares of the unbloody Sacrifice that it is “not different, and… is one and the same” (pgs 67-68). In that same place it cites St. John Chrysostom as showing, “in many places,” the truth of this. One example, from his Commentary on Hebrews 9:24-26, paragraph 3: “He is Himself then both victim and Priest and sacrifice. For if it had not been so, and it had been necessary to offer many sacrifices, He must have been many times crucified.” And on Hebrews 10:2-9, paragraph 6: “For we always offer the same, not one sheep now and tomorrow another, but always the same thing: so that the sacrifice is one. And yet by this reasoning, since the offering is made in many places, are there many Christs? But Christ is one everywhere, being complete here and complete there also, one Body. As then while offered in many places, He is one body and not many bodies; so also [He is] one sacrifice. He is our High Priest, who offered the sacrifice that cleanses us. That we offer now also, which was then offered, which cannot be exhausted.”[68] Which followers of Luther Dositheus has in mind is unclear; Confessional Lutherans deny impanation and the Capernaitic eating of the Body of Christ. See Formula of Concord, Epitome (1576), Article 7.14-15, 42; Solid Declaration (1577), Article 7.14-15, 64.[69] This is not a hocus pocus change for, as the Confession clearly affirms, the material species and form of the bread and wine remain. In other words, the material, which is to say the matter, undergoes no change. Moreover, no violence is done to the substance of the bread, nor is it annihilated, but is trans-substantiated.[70] In light of the contemporary scientific understanding of matter, it is important to understand what the doctrine of transubstantiation, as a philosophical explanation, is and is not saying. In contemporary terms, a thing “is” essentially what its matter determines, for people today generally assume a stance of philosophical materialism. Thus bread, according to this view, is only its material properties, i.e. its matter. But this corresponds to what the Confession describes as its accidents. Today, the substance/accidents distinction is not typically applied in reflection on what a thing is, and so when asking what a thing “is,” the answer is usually in terms of its material properties, for matter is typically understood as the ground of being. However, in substance/accidents ontology, the question of “is” finds its answer in its substance, which is not per se the material substance or properties. What we today understand material substance to be is what the substance/accidents distinction calls the accidents of the “what it is.” The matter instantiates a substance but is not identical with that substance. Thus, for example, when the “is” of the bread changes to the Body, it is not a claim that the molecular structure which constitutes the material accidents of the bread changes, but that the “is” of the bread changes to the “is” of Christ’s Body; the accidents, i.e. what we today call the material substance, remains constant. As such, only the “is” of Christ’s Body remains, for the “is” of the bread is transformed, leaving its material substance, i.e. its accidents, so that it can still be spoken of as bread in this limited material sense, whereas in reality it is Christ’s Body. See also St. Peter Mogila’s Catechism, Question 107, who states that the Body of Christ is “under the clothing of bread and wine.”[71] In other words, the material accidents, which are handled and ingested, are the accidents of the bread and wine, not the accidents of Christ’s Body; in handling these, the very Body and Body of Christ are consumed. Christ’s Body and Blood are not present as per accidents, but as per substance. This doctrine excludes materialistic reductionism as a viable worldview.[72] “For it is not the justice of the judge that is the cause of punishment, but the action of the evil doer” (Chapter 1 of the Jerusalem Synod, Against the Third Question, pg 52).[73] This not only denies that there is a third place, i.e. Purgatory, but that such deliverance is not granted to the faithless, but to the faithful who have left while still, tragically, clinging to some sin. The “satisfaction” (ἱκανοποίησιν) is granted principally through the Cross, which is to say the Eucharistic and unbloody Sacrifice, therefore demonstrating the Gospel-centered nature of such satisfaction, and not through the works or the suffering of the one needing deliverance. They do not satisfy for themselves or for their own sins. Moreover, as among the faithful, faith is not being supplied to them where none was, but as one clinging to sin it is as though their will was frozen in its trust in Christ, and so the power of Christ works in their already-present faith, through the Cross and the intercessions of the Church, to deliver them from their bondage to the clung-to sin.[74] What this is not saying is that laity are forbidden from reading the Scriptures, but that all due faithful preparation is required to read the Scriptures safely and with spiritual profit. Nothing bars anyone from meeting the above listed prerequisites, even among Orthodox youth. The holy Fathers regularly exhort the faithful to read the Scriptures, to which all should aspire.[75] Those who stubbornly refuse proper disciplines of reading, whether through excessive oversimplification, or reducing all the genres of Scripture to gross literalism, or insisting on reading into Scripture conclusions contrary to orthodox faith, are forbidden from reading until they can meet the minimum standards of faithful reading.[76] The simplicity of Christian faith is such that one need not be a Bible scholar in order to be saved. In this it is worthwhile to remember that prior to the modern era the rate of literacy was typically much lower, most people living in oral cultures.[77] Notice that it is not due to any clericalism that the undisciplined are forbidden, but that they simply mature in the faith to the degree that reading is profitable to them.[78] Historical theology, which charts the long course the Church has waged against innumerable errors and distortions in theology, is ample enough evidence that examination of the Scriptures requires the faithful, prayerful, and disciplined synthesis of the whole counsel of God, and cannot be rashly taken up without injury. See also St. Philaret’s Longer Catechism, Question 56.[79] Canons 59-60.[80] This is an ecclesiological argument, holding that it is only through the authority granted to the Church, as the Bride of Christ, that holy Scripture, her Bridegroom’s Word, can be distinguished from what is spurious, such that to reject what the Church holds to be Scripture is to reject the very principle and mechanism by which any Scripture is identified as Scripture at all. Though God’s Word is internally self-authenticating, it requires among men an authoritative public means by which it can be distinguished from the spurious, which means is the Church. The Church thus does not “make” Scripture to “be” Scripture, but for the sake of the Body declares it to be what it factually is in and of itself, such that without the Church Scripture is lost.[81] On this see also St. Philaret’s Longer Catechism, Questions 31-35.[82] In allusion to the manger out of which the irrational animals eat their food. -Translator’s note[83] These, together with the Octoechos and the Pentecostarion, are among the principle liturgical books containing the hymns used during services throughout the Church Year.[84] The Greek name, Θηκαρᾶ, is uncertain, but may refer to a collection of hymns that bore this person’s name, either as author or compiler.[85] The fact of the charism of the monastic calling is rooted in such teachings of Christ as in Matthew 19:12, and St. Paul in 1 Corinthians 7:8-9, 32-35, 38, and is a profound spiritual reality of the Church. The Calvinist criticism of the charism of monasticism is thus not only an affront to the Church, but also to the Word of God and to the Holy Spirit, who graces some with a call to the celibate monastic life. The Calvinist rejection of monasticism is therefore a much more significant problem than simply a foregoing of its practice.[86] Note that this is not a rigid or extreme asceticism, nor a perfecting through the flesh (cf. Galatians 3:3), nor a replacement for grace, but a Spirit-led means by which one mortifies the passions, engages in spiritual warfare, and co-operates with grace, in order to look more readily from self to God, wean from self-will, and diminish reliance on and attachment to sensualism. It not only aids the body, but strengthens character.[87] The raised floor or platform on which the holy Altar sits within the Sanctuary of the Church, which can refer to the space of the Sanctuary itself. [143] Note that particular sinful acts arise from these more fundamental sinful dispositions, of pride, greed, lust, etc. The acts are therefore sin-full because they carry and express the energy of the more fundamental sinful disposition. The fundamental sin is, thus, in fact, that more fundamental disposition which gives rise to or energizes particular sinful actions. The mortal sin being like the trunk of a tree, sinful acts are like so many branches or leaves.[144] Other terms which can describe the state of this sin are spiritual dullness, indifference, apathy, laziness, sluggishness, unresponsiveness.[145] In other words, theological fatalism.[146] See footnote for Question 16.[147] Job 42:7-10: And it was so, that after the Lord had spoken these words unto Job, the Lord said to Eliphaz the Temanite, My wrath is kindled against thee, and against thy two friends: for ye have not spoken of me the thing that is right, as my servant Job hath. Therefore take unto you now seven bullocks and seven rams, and go to my servant Job, and offer up for yourselves a burnt offering; and my servant Job shall pray for you: for him will I accept: lest I deal with you after your folly, in that ye have not spoken of me the thing which is right, like my servant Job. Lest therefore we should ignore the fullness of this observance we make it plain to the faithful that after the priests have gone to the Altar for Vespers on Saturdays (according to the prevailing custom) no one shall kneel in prayer until the evening of Sunday, at which time after the entrance for compline, again with bended knees we offer our prayers to the Lord. For taking the night after the Sabbath, which was the forerunner of our Lord's resurrection, we begin from it to sing in the spirit hymns to God, leading our feast out of darkness into light, and thus during an entire day and night, we celebrate the Resurrection.”[150] A fact to be considered when endorsing political parties.[151] To such must be added also the careful use, regulation, and/or rejection, in whole or in part, of such modern electronic devices as television, video games, and the internet.[152] “So whatever you wish that others would do to you, do also to them, for this is the Law and the Prophets” (Matthew 7:12).
